  ;NREUM.info={beacon:"bam.nr-data.net",errorBeacon:"bam.nr-data.net",licenseKey:"7fc7d5aa08",applicationID:"34219174",sa:1}</script><script type="text/javascript" data-reactid="4">var _min={_publisher:"MIN-40200"};!function(){var e=document.createElement("script");e.src=document.location.protocol+"//snippet.minute.ly/mi-time-prod.js",e.type="text/javascript",e.async=!0,e.onload=e.onreadystatechange=function(){e.setAttribute("crossorigin","crossorigin")};var t=document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0];t.appendChild(e)}();</script><title data-react-helmet="true" data-reactid="5">Men&#x27;s Rights Activism, A Voice For Men, Elliot Rodger, Misogyny | Time.com</title><meta data-react-helmet="true" charset="utf-8" data-reactid="6"/><meta data-react-helmet="true" http-equiv="&#x27;X-UA-Compatible&#x27;, content=&#x27;IE=edge&#x27;" data-reactid="7"/><meta data-react-helmet="true" http-equiv="&#x27;Content-Type&#x27;, content=&#x27;text/html; charset=utf-8&#x27;" data-reactid="8"/><meta data-react-helmet="true" name="description" content="A growing movement driven by misogyny and misinformation is appealing to frustrated men struggling with changing definitions of masculinity." data-reactid="9"/><meta data-react-helmet="true" name="keywords" content="AVoiceForMen, elliot rodger, gender, Men\&#x27;s Rights, Misogyny, MRA, politics, sexism, U.C. A men&#x27;s fitness columnist on why they should walk away." data-reactid="18"/><meta data-react-helmet="true" property="og:image" content="https://timedotcom.files.wordpress.com/2014/05/bowvswhcyaa1pm1.jpg?w=720" data-reactid="19"/><meta data-react-helmet="true" property="fb:app_id" content="53177223193" data-reactid="20"/><meta data-react-helmet="true" name="twitter:site" content="@TIME" data-reactid="21"/><meta data-react-helmet="true" name="twitter:title" content="The Toxic Appeal of the Men&#x27;s Rights Movement " data-reactid="22"/><meta data-react-helmet="true" name="twitter:description" content="A growing movement driven by misogyny and resentment is pulling in frustrated men struggling with changing definitions of masculinity. A men&#x27;s fitness columnist on why they should walk away." data-reactid="23"/><meta data-react-helmet="true" name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" data-reactid="24"/><meta data-react-helmet="true" property="article:author" content="http://time.com/author/james-s-fell-2/" data-reactid="25"/><link data-react-helmet="true" rel="canonical" href="http://time.com/134152/the-toxic-appeal-of-the-mens-rights-movement/" data-reactid="26"/><link data-react-helmet="true" rel="amphtml" href="http://amp.timeinc.net/time/134152/the-toxic-appeal-of-the-mens-rights-movement/?source=dam" data-reactid="27"/><script data-react-helmet="true" type="application/ld+json" data-reactid="28">{"@context":"http://schema.org","@type":"NewsArticle","mainEntityOfPage":{"@type":"WebPage","@id":"http://time.com/134152/the-toxic-appeal-of-the-mens-rights-movement/"},"headline":"The Toxic Appeal of the Men&#x27;s Rights Movement","url":"http://time.com/134152/the-toxic-appeal-of-the-mens-rights-movement/","dateCreated":"2014-05-29T11:00:05","datePublished":"2014-05-29T11:00:05","dateModified":"2014-05-29T04:00:39","creator":["James S. Fell"],"keywords":["avoiceformen","elliot-rodger","gender","mens-rights","misogyny","mra","politics","sexism","u-c-santa-barbara"],"author":["James S. Fell"],"publisher":{"@type":"Organization","name":"Time","logo":{"@type":"ImageObject","url":"http://assets.time.com/brandlogos/time.png","height":42,"width":128}},"articleBody":"Imagine a kid who got a cone with three scoops of ice cream in it. Good flavors, too. Like peanut-butter chocolate, plus a scoop of cookie dough. In a waffle cone. And then this child whines about the lack of chocolate sprinkles on top. Welcome to the men&rsquo;s rights movement. Wait, what? Men&rsquo;s rights? That&rsquo;s a thing? Yes, it&rsquo;s a thing, and while there are certain legitimate aspects to men&rsquo;s rights activism, or MRA, it&rsquo;s overwhelmingly a toxic slew of misogyny. This world of resentment and hate speech has been brought to light in recent days as we learned about the vitriolic forum posts and videos left behind by Elliot Rodger, the 22 year-old accused of killing six people in Santa Barbara last week. But it&#8217;s hard to comprehend from Roger&#8217;s delusional rants how potent the movement&#8217;s message can be for ordinary men. MRAs believe the traditionally oppressed groups have somehow seized control and taken away their white male privilege. They tap into fear and insecurity and turn it into blame and rage. Often the leaders of these groups are men who feel as though they got screwed in a divorce. They quote all sorts of statistics about child custody and unfair alimony payments, because in their minds, the single mother who has to choose between feeding the kids or paying the rent is a myth. They believe passionately in their own victimhood and their creed goes something like this: Women are trying to keep us down, usurp all our power, taking away what it means to be a man. One popular MRA site is AVoiceForMen.com, with a mission to &ldquo;expose misandry on all levels in our culture&rdquo; and &ldquo;denounce the institution of marriage as unsafe and unsuitable for modern men&rdquo; as well as &ldquo;promote an end to chivalry in any form or fashion&rdquo; and &ldquo;educate men and boys about the threats they face in feminist governance.&rdquo; They also want an &ldquo;end to rape hysteria&rdquo; and promote &ldquo;civil disobedience.&rdquo; In their defense, AVFM does support nonviolence, but with all the inflammatory rhetoric, do readers always take heed? There are Reddit threads and other Internet forums dedicated to men&rsquo;s rights, and the language and vitriol towards women and especially towards feminism is appalling. Any messages of nonviolence seem lost in the hate mongering. These groups spew logically faulty statistics about the prevalence of male rape and spousal abuse, and how there really is no glass ceiling or pay inequality, and general complaints about how &ldquo;that bitch got my promotion because she has a uterus.&rdquo; Men&rsquo;s Rights Canada made headlines again recently with their classless response to an anti-sexual assault campaign called &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t be that guy.&rdquo; Posters went up across the nation implying women aren&rsquo;t punished enough for infanticide, stating, &ldquo;Women can stop baby dumping. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t be that girl.&rdquo; This was a follow up of the same campaign from last year alleging many women made false rape accusations because they felt guilty over a one-night stand. As a white man who writes about fitness, I&rsquo;m very aware of the pressures on men and the many ways that these kinds of hateful messages reach my audience, both overt like the Canada ads and the less blatant claims of male victimhood in mainstream media. It&#8217;s clear that the definition of masculinity is in flux, and for some men that&rsquo;s frustrating, especially with near-pornographic ad campaigns promoting women as objects of sexual conquest. And while there are aspects of MRA that are worth bringing to light, as a movement it can suck a good man down a rabbit hole of resentment. It is backward-looking and pining for good old days that never were. Are there some problems with specific instances of unequal treatment? Yes. Is there some anti-male sentiment out there? Yeah, that happens too. But turning these issues into a movement is laughable. It is a like a multi-millionaire who whines that a tax loophole was closed and he&rsquo;s losing 0.5% of his annual income. Men, especially white men, aren&rsquo;t marginalized, we aren&rsquo;t under attack, and we not in danger of losing the overwhelming privileges society bestows upon us for having pale skin and a penis. However, MRAs have been described as whining children by the women they call &ldquo;feminist bitches.&rdquo; So to any man who feels like he&rsquo;s getting caught up in such a movement because they feel emasculated or are just having trouble relating to women and perhaps sympathizing with Elliot Rodger, I will tell you this: Life isn&rsquo;t fair. Life is NOT fair. Women will judge you. Some will judge you based on your appearance, your height, your width, you genitalia, your wealth, your car, your clothes, your acne. In other words, they will judge you the exact same way you judge them. Fell is a syndicated fitness columnist for the Chicago Tribune and Los Angeles Times. He blogs at http://www.SixPackAbs.com. You can follow him data-reactid="223"></path></svg></a></div></div></div><div class="row" data-reactid="224"><div class="column small-12" data-reactid="225"></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><article class="row" data-reactid="226"><p class="column small-12 medium-10 medium-offset-1 large-offset-2  text size-1x-large line-height-large _10M0Ygc4" data-reactid="227"><!-- react-text: 228 -->Imagine a kid who got a cone with three scoops of ice cream in it. Good flavors, too. Like peanut-butter chocolate, plus a scoop of cookie dough. In a waffle cone. And then this child whines about the lack of chocolate sprinkles on top.<!-- /react-text --></p><p class="column small-12 medium-10 medium-offset-1 large-offset-2  text size-1x-large line-height-large _10M0Ygc4" data-reactid="229"><!-- react-text: 230 -->Welcome to the mens rights movement.<!-- /react-text --></p><div data-reactid="231"></div><p class="column small-12 medium-10 medium-offset-1 large-offset-2  text size-1x-large line-height-large _10M0Ygc4" data-reactid="232"><!-- react-text: 233 -->Wait, what? <!-- /react-text --><i data-reactid="234">Mens</i><!-- react-text: 235 --> rights? Thats a thing? Yes, its a thing, and while there are certain legitimate aspects to mens rights activism, or MRA, its overwhelmingly a toxic slew of misogyny. This world of resentment and hate speech has been brought to light in recent days as we learned about the vitriolic forum posts and videos left behind by Elliot Rodger, the 22 year-old accused of killing six people in Santa Barbara last week. But it&#x27;s hard to comprehend from Roger&#x27;s delusional rants how potent the movement&#x27;s message can be for ordinary men.<!-- /react-text --></p><p class="column small-12 medium-10 medium-offset-1 large-offset-2  text size-1x-large line-height-large _10M0Ygc4" data-reactid="236"><!-- react-text: 237 -->MRAs believe the traditionally oppressed groups have somehow seized control and taken away their white male privilege. They tap into fear and insecurity and turn it into blame and rage. Often the leaders of these groups are men who feel as though they got screwed in a divorce. They quote all sorts of statistics about child custody and unfair alimony payments, because in their minds, the single mother who has to choose between feeding the kids or paying the rent is a myth. They believe passionately in their own victimhood and their creed goes something like this: <!-- /react-text --><i data-reactid="238">Women are trying to keep us down, usurp all our power, taking away what it means to be a man.</i></p><div class="medium-offset-1 dont-print _2R4B45U9" data-reactid="239"><div class="show-for-large" data-reactid="240"><h2 class="text font-accent all-caps lWcQivxu" data-reactid="241">Related</h2><div class="_3RD_3EaM" data-reactid="242"><div class="column small-12" data-reactid="243"><div class="row" style="height:149px;" data-reactid="244"><a target="_self" href="/4716860/dictionary-com-new-words-alt-right-black-lives-matter/" class="_2S9ChopF" data-reactid="245"><img class="b-lazy " title="Dictionary" data-reactid="246"/></a></div></div><div class="column small-12" data-reactid="247"><div class="row _2WEbB5ya" data-reactid="248"><a class="text font-accent color-brand href="/4716860/dictionary-com-new-words-alt-right-black-lives-matter/" data-reactid="250">Dictionary.com Adds 'Alt-Right,' 'Slay' and 'Black Lives Matter'</a></div><div class="_1xP-VEhr" data-reactid="251"><div class="_1X3LqGF7" data-reactid="252"><a href="http://time.com/" data-reactid="253"><svg itemprop='logo' height='100%' preserveAspectRatio='xMinYMin meet' viewBox='0 0 98 32'><path d='M3 2.36h23.67v5.4h-.43l.05-3.46h-9.35v24.36h2.7v.8H10.4v-.8h2.5V4.3H3.45v3.46H3v-5.4zM27.8 2.36h9.3v.75h-2.65v25.56h2.64v.8h-9.3v-.8h2.7V3.1h-2.7v-.74zM45.3 2.3h1.78l8.7 20.9 5.35h-.37l-.17-3.3-13.4-.15v10.7h11.4l.27-2.5h.5c-.12 2.28-.12 4.5 0 6.76h-.5l-.27-2.58h-11.4V27.7h13.9l.04-3.68h.38v5.45h-20.9v-.76l2.7-.04V3.16l-2.7-.05v-.74z'/></svg></a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="hide-for-large" data-reactid="254"><div class="_2Kl5uoWP" data-reactid="255"><div class="column small-12" data-reactid="256"><div class="row _3CTK81ZD" data-reactid="257"><a class="text font-accent size-1x-small all-caps" href="http://time.com/tag/language" data-reactid="258">language</a></div></div><div class="column small-12" data-reactid="259"><div class="row _2OkxEqQr" data-reactid="260"><a class="text size-2x-small hedBUxnp" href="/4716860/dictionary-com-new-words-alt-right-black-lives-matter/" data-reactid="261">Dictionary.com Adds 'Alt-Right,' 'Slay' and 'Black Lives Matter'</a></div><div class="y3ubIuJm" data-reactid="262"><div class="_1X3LqGF7" data-reactid="263"><a href="http://time.com/" data-reactid="264"><svg itemprop='logo' height='100%' preserveAspectRatio='xMinYMin meet' viewBox='0 0 98 32'><path d='M3 2.36h23.67v5.4h-.43l.05-3.46h-9.35v24.36h2.7v.8H10.4v-.8h2.5V4.3H3.45v3.46H3v-5.4zM27.8 2.36h9.3v.75h-2.65v25.56h2.64v.8h-9.3v-.8h2.7V3.1h-2.7v-.74zM45.3 2.3h1.78l8.7 5.35h-.37l-.17-3.3-13.4-.15v10.7h11.4l.27-2.5h.5c-.12 2.28-.12 4.5 0 6.76h-.5l-.27-2.58h-11.4V27.7h13.9l.04-3.68h.38v5.45h-20.9v-.76l2.7-.04V3.16l-2.7-.05v-.74z'/></svg></a></div></div></div></div></div></div><p class="column small-12 medium-10 medium-offset-1 large-offset-2  text size-1x-large line-height-large _10M0Ygc4" data-reactid="265"><!-- react-text: 266 -->One popular MRA site is AVoiceForMen.com, with a mission to expose misandry on all levels in our culture and denounce the institution of marriage as unsafe and unsuitable for modern men as well as promote an end to chivalry in any form or fashion and educate men and boys about the threats they face in feminist governance. They also want an end to rape hysteria and promote civil disobedience. In their defense, AVFM does support nonviolence, but with all the inflammatory rhetoric, do readers always take heed?<!-- /react-text --></p><p class="column small-12 medium-10 medium-offset-1 large-offset-2  text size-1x-large line-height-large _10M0Ygc4" data-reactid="267"><!-- react-text: 268 -->There are Reddit threads and other Internet forums dedicated to mens rights, and the language and vitriol towards women and especially towards feminism is appalling. Any messages of nonviolence seem lost in the hate mongering. These groups spew logically faulty statistics about the prevalence of male rape and spousal abuse, and how there really is no glass ceiling or pay inequality, and general complaints about how that bitch got my promotion because she has a uterus.<!-- /react-text --></p><p class="column small-12 medium-10 medium-offset-1 large-offset-2  text size-1x-large line-height-large _10M0Ygc4" data-reactid="269"><!-- react-text: 270 -->Mens Rights Canada <!-- /react-text --><a rel=" noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" href="http://o.canada.com/news/mens-rights-posters-dont-be-that-girl-423582" data-reactid="271"><!-- react-text: 272 -->made headlines again recently<!-- /react-text --></a><!-- react-text: 273 --> with their classless response to an anti-sexual assault campaign called Dont be <!-- /react-text --><i data-reactid="274">that</i><!-- react-text: 275 --> guy. Posters went up across the nation implying women arent punished enough for infanticide, stating,  Women can stop baby dumping. <!-- /react-text --><a rel=" noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" href="http://www.huffingtonpost.ca/2013/07/12/dont-be-that-girl-mens-rights-edmonton_n_3587808.html" data-reactid="276"><!-- react-text: 277 -->Dont be <!-- /react-text --><i data-reactid="278">that</i><!-- react-text: 279 --> girl.<!-- /react-text --></a><!-- react-text: 280 --> This was a follow up of the same campaign from last year alleging many women made false rape accusations because they felt guilty over a one-night stand.<!-- /react-text --></p><p class="column small-12 medium-10 medium-offset-1 large-offset-2  text size-1x-large line-height-large _10M0Ygc4" data-reactid="281"><!-- react-text: 282 -->As a white man who writes about fitness, Im very aware of the pressures on men and the many ways that these kinds of hateful messages reach my audience, both overt like the Canada ads and the less blatant claims of male victimhood in mainstream media. It&#x27;s clear that the definition of masculinity is in flux, and for some men thats frustrating, especially with near-pornographic ad campaigns promoting women as objects of sexual conquest. And while there are aspects of MRA that are worth bringing to light, as a movement it can suck a good man down a rabbit hole of resentment. It is backward-looking and pining for good old days that never were.<!-- /react-text --></p><p class="column small-12 medium-10 medium-offset-1 large-offset-2  text size-1x-large line-height-large _10M0Ygc4" data-reactid="283"><!-- react-text: 284 -->Are there some problems with specific instances of unequal treatment? Yes. Is there some anti-male sentiment out there? Yeah, that happens too. But turning these issues into a movement is laughable. It is a like a multi-millionaire who whines that a tax loophole was closed and hes losing 0.5% of his annual income.<!-- /react-text --></p><p class="column small-12 medium-10 medium-offset-1 large-offset-2  text size-1x-large line-height-large _10M0Ygc4" data-reactid="285"><!-- react-text: 286 -->Men, especially white men, arent marginalized, we arent under attack, and we not in danger of losing the overwhelming privileges society bestows upon us for having pale skin and a penis. However, MRAs <!-- /react-text --><i data-reactid="287">have</i><!-- react-text: 288 --> been described as<!-- /react-text --><i data-reactid="289"> </i><!-- react-text: 290 -->whining children by the women they call feminist bitches.<!-- /react-text --></p><p class="column small-12 medium-10 medium-offset-1 large-offset-2  text size-1x-large line-height-large _10M0Ygc4" data-reactid="291"><!-- react-text: 292 -->So to any man who feels like hes getting caught up in such a movement because they feel emasculated or are just having trouble relating to women and perhaps sympathizing with Elliot Rodger, I will tell you this: Life isnt fair. Life is NOT fair.<!-- /react-text --></p><p class="column small-12 medium-10 medium-offset-1 large-offset-2  text size-1x-large line-height-large _10M0Ygc4" data-reactid="293"><!-- react-text: 294 -->Women will judge you. Some will judge you based on your appearance, your height, your width, you genitalia, your wealth, your car, your clothes, your acne. In other words, they will judge you the exact same way you judge them.<!-- /react-text --></p><p class="column small-12 medium-10 medium-offset-1 large-offset-2 end text size-1x-large line-height-large _10M0Ygc4" data-reactid="295"><em data-reactid="296">Fell is a syndicated fitness columnist for the Chicago Tribune and Los Angeles Times. He blogs at www.SixPackAbs.com. You can follow him @bodyforwife</em></p></article></div></div></div><div class="column show-for-large dont-print _2Xqd5uis" data-reactid="297"><div data-reactid="298"></div></div></div></div><div Toxic Appeal of the Men's Rights Movement"},"content":{"rendered":"\u003Cp\u003EImagine a kid who got a cone with three scoops of ice cream in it. Good flavors, too. Like peanut-butter chocolate, plus a scoop of cookie dough. In a waffle cone. And then this child whines about the lack of chocolate sprinkles on top.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWelcome to the men&rsquo;s rights movement.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWait, what? \u003Ci\u003EMen&rsquo;s\u003C\u002Fi\u003E rights? That&rsquo;s a thing? Yes, it&rsquo;s a thing, and while there are certain legitimate aspects to men&rsquo;s rights activism, or MRA, it&rsquo;s overwhelmingly a toxic slew of misogyny. This world of resentment and hate speech has been brought to light in recent days as we learned about the vitriolic forum posts and videos left behind by Elliot Rodger, the 22 year-old accused of killing six people in Santa Barbara last week. But it&#8217;s hard to comprehend from Roger&#8217;s delusional rants how potent the movement&#8217;s message can be for ordinary men.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EMRAs believe the traditionally oppressed groups have somehow seized control and taken away their white male privilege. They tap into fear and insecurity and turn it into blame and rage. Often the leaders of these groups are men who feel as though they got screwed in a divorce. They quote all sorts of statistics about child custody and unfair alimony payments, because in their minds, the single mother who has to choose between feeding the kids or paying the rent is a myth. They believe passionately in their own victimhood and their creed goes something like this: \u003Ci\u003EWomen are trying to keep us down, usurp all our power, taking away what it means to be a man.\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOne popular MRA site is AVoiceForMen.com, with a mission to &ldquo;expose misandry on all levels in our culture&rdquo; and &ldquo;denounce the institution of marriage as unsafe and unsuitable for modern men&rdquo; as well as &ldquo;promote an end to chivalry in any form or fashion&rdquo; and &ldquo;educate men and boys about the threats they face in feminist governance.&rdquo; They also want an &ldquo;end to rape hysteria&rdquo; and promote &ldquo;civil disobedience.&rdquo; In their defense, AVFM does support nonviolence, but with all the inflammatory rhetoric, do readers always take heed?\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThere are Reddit threads and other Internet forums dedicated to men&rsquo;s rights, and the language and vitriol towards women and especially towards feminism is appalling. Any messages of nonviolence seem lost in the hate mongering. These groups spew logically faulty statistics about the prevalence of male rape and spousal abuse, and how there really is no glass ceiling or pay inequality, and general complaints about how &ldquo;that bitch got my promotion because she has a uterus.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EMen&rsquo;s Rights Canada \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fo.canada.com\u002Fnews\u002Fmens-rights-posters-dont-be-that-girl-423582\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E\u003Cspan class=\"s3\"\u003Emade headlines again recently\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E with their classless response to an anti-sexual assault campaign called &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t be \u003Ci\u003Ethat\u003C\u002Fi\u003E guy.&rdquo; Posters went up across the nation implying women aren&rsquo;t punished enough for infanticide, stating, &ldquo;\u003Cspan class=\"s2\"\u003EWomen can stop baby dumping. &ldquo;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.huffingtonpost.ca\u002F2013\u002F07\u002F12\u002Fdont-be-that-girl-mens-rights-edmonton_n_3587808.html\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E\u003Cspan class=\"s3\"\u003EDon&rsquo;t be \u003Ci\u003Ethat\u003C\u002Fi\u003E girl.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E This was a follow up of the same campaign from last year alleging many women made false rape accusations because they felt guilty over a one-night stand.\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAs a white man who writes about fitness, I&rsquo;m very aware of the pressures on men and the many ways that these kinds of hateful messages reach my audience, both overt like the Canada ads and the less blatant claims of male victimhood in mainstream media. It&#8217;s clear that the definition of masculinity is in flux, and for some men that&rsquo;s frustrating, especially with near-pornographic ad campaigns promoting women as objects of sexual conquest. And while there are aspects of MRA that are worth bringing to light, as a movement it can suck a good man down a rabbit hole of resentment. It is backward-looking and pining for good old days that never were.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAre there some problems with specific instances of unequal treatment? Yes. Is there some anti-male sentiment out there? Yeah, that happens too. But turning these issues into a movement is laughable. It is a like a multi-millionaire who whines that a tax loophole was closed and he&rsquo;s losing 0.5% of his annual income.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EMen, especially white men, aren&rsquo;t marginalized, we aren&rsquo;t under attack, and we not in danger of losing the overwhelming privileges society bestows upon us for having pale skin and a penis. However, MRAs \u003Ci\u003Ehave\u003C\u002Fi\u003E been described as\u003Ci\u003E \u003C\u002Fi\u003Ewhining children by the women they call &ldquo;feminist bitches.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESo to any man who feels like he&rsquo;s getting caught up in such a movement because they feel emasculated or are just having trouble relating to women and perhaps sympathizing with Elliot Rodger, I will tell you this: Life isn&rsquo;t fair. Life is NOT fair.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWomen will judge you. Some will judge you based on your appearance, your height, your width, you genitalia, your wealth, your car, your clothes, your acne. In other words, they will judge you the exact same way you judge them.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EFell is a syndicated fitness columnist for the Chicago Tribune and Los Angeles Times. He blogs at www.SixPackAbs.com. You can follow him @bodyforwife"]}]}],"plain":"Imagine a kid who got a cone with three scoops of ice cream in it. Good flavors, too. Like peanut-butter chocolate, plus a scoop of cookie dough. In a waffle cone. And then this child whines about the lack of chocolate sprinkles on top. Welcome to the men&rsquo;s rights movement. Wait, what? Men&rsquo;s rights? That&rsquo;s a thing? Yes, it&rsquo;s a thing, and while there are certain legitimate aspects to men&rsquo;s rights activism, or MRA, it&rsquo;s overwhelmingly a toxic slew of misogyny. This world of resentment and hate speech has been brought to light in recent days as we learned about the vitriolic forum posts and videos left behind by Elliot Rodger, the 22 year-old accused of killing six people in Santa Barbara last week. But it&#8217;s hard to comprehend from Roger&#8217;s delusional rants how potent the movement&#8217;s message can be for ordinary men. MRAs believe the traditionally oppressed groups have somehow seized control and taken away their white male privilege. They tap into fear and insecurity and turn it into blame and rage. Often the leaders of these groups are men who feel as though they got screwed in a divorce. They quote all sorts of statistics about child custody and unfair alimony payments, because in their minds, the single mother who has to choose between feeding the kids or paying the rent is a myth. They believe passionately in their own victimhood and their creed goes something like this: Women are trying to keep us down, usurp all our power, taking away what it means to be a man. One popular MRA site is AVoiceForMen.com, with a mission to &ldquo;expose misandry on all levels in our culture&rdquo; and &ldquo;denounce the institution of marriage as unsafe and unsuitable for modern men&rdquo; as well as &ldquo;promote an end to chivalry in any form or fashion&rdquo; and &ldquo;educate men and boys about the threats they face in feminist governance.&rdquo; They also want an &ldquo;end to rape hysteria&rdquo; and promote &ldquo;civil disobedience.&rdquo; In their defense, AVFM does support nonviolence, but with all the inflammatory rhetoric, do readers always take heed? There are Reddit threads and other Internet forums dedicated to men&rsquo;s rights, and the language and vitriol towards women and especially towards feminism is appalling. Any messages of nonviolence seem lost in the hate mongering. These groups spew logically faulty statistics about the prevalence of male rape and spousal abuse, and how there really is no glass ceiling or pay inequality, and general complaints about how &ldquo;that bitch got my promotion because she has a uterus.&rdquo; Men&rsquo;s Rights Canada made headlines again recently with their classless response to an anti-sexual assault campaign called &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t be that guy.&rdquo; Posters went up across the nation implying women aren&rsquo;t punished enough for infanticide, stating, &ldquo;Women can stop baby dumping. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t be that girl.&rdquo; This was a follow up of the same campaign from last year alleging many women made false rape accusations because they felt guilty over a one-night stand. As a white man who writes about fitness, I&rsquo;m very aware of the pressures on men and the many ways that these kinds of hateful messages reach my audience, both overt like the Canada ads and the less blatant claims of male victimhood in mainstream media. It&#8217;s clear that the definition of masculinity is in flux, and for some men that&rsquo;s frustrating, especially with near-pornographic ad campaigns promoting women as objects of sexual conquest. And while there are aspects of MRA that are worth bringing to light, as a movement it can suck a good man down a rabbit hole of resentment. It is backward-looking and pining for good old days that never were. Are there some problems with specific instances of unequal treatment? Yes. Is there some anti-male sentiment out there? Yeah, that happens too. But turning these issues into a movement is laughable. It is a like a multi-millionaire who whines that a tax loophole was closed and he&rsquo;s losing 0.5% of his annual income. Men, especially white men, aren&rsquo;t marginalized, we aren&rsquo;t under attack, and we not in danger of losing the overwhelming privileges society bestows upon us for having pale skin and a penis. However, MRAs have been described as whining children by the women they call &ldquo;feminist bitches.&rdquo; So to any man who feels like he&rsquo;s getting caught up in such a movement because they feel emasculated or are just having trouble relating to women and perhaps sympathizing with Elliot Rodger, I will tell you this: Life isn&rsquo;t fair. Life is NOT fair. Women will judge you. Some will judge you based on your appearance, your height, your width, you genitalia, your wealth, your car, your clothes, your acne. In other words, they will judge you the exact same way you judge them. Fell is a syndicated fitness columnist for the Chicago Tribune and Los Angeles Times. He blogs at http:\u002F\u002Fwww.SixPackAbs.com. You can follow him @bodyforwife"},"excerpt":{"rendered":"\u003Cp\u003EA growing movement driven by misogyny and resentment is pulling in frustrated men struggling with changing definitions of masculinity. A men&#8217;s fitness columnist on why they should walk He had won a \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwherewestand.gettyimages.com\u002Fgi_grants\u002Fgrants-for-editorial-photography\u002F#recipients\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EGetty Images Editorial Grant\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, an \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.alexiafoundation.org\u002Fstories\u002Fin-the-shadow-of-kolkata-slumlife-in-india\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EAlexia Foundation\u003C\u002Fa\u003E award and the \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fvisura.co\u002Fgrant\u002Fwinners2016pj\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EVisura Photojournalism Grant\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, highlights in a career that began about three years ago, when Datta was in Kolkata, India, producing a photographic project on violence in the city&rsquo;s sex industry. Now, however, he finds himself at the center of the latest photo-manipulation scandal to have ensnared the photojournalism community &mdash; a sorry trend that has also touched \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Flens.blogs.nytimes.com\u002F2013\u002F02\u002F22\u002Fa-prize-winning-ethics-lesson\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EPaolo Pellegrin,\u003C\u002Fa\u003E \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002F4351725\u002Fsteve-mccurry-not-photojournalist\u002F\"\u003ESteve McCurry\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fworld.time.com\u002F2014\u002F01\u002F22\u002Fap-fires-photographer-altered-photo\u002F\"\u003ENarciso Contreras\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002F3728436\u002Fworld-press-photo-upholds-controversial-decision\u002F\"\u003EGiovanni Troilo,\u003C\u002Fa\u003E among others.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAs was first revealed by \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fpetapixel.com\u002F2017\u002F05\u002F03\u002Fphotographer-souvid-datta-appears-plagiarized-mary-ellen-mark\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EPetaPixel\u003C\u002Fa\u003E on Wednesday, it was during the course of that project, as he followed a girl in one of the town&rsquo;s brothels, that Datta turned to Photoshop. When the girl&#8217;s mentor asked not to be photographed, he cloned out a subject from a photograph by the legendary \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002F3901772\u002Fmary-ellen-mark-charles-harbutt\u002F\"\u003EMary Ellen Mark\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and pasted it in his own photograph. When he uploaded the image to his blog, there was no indication that the work was not entirely his own. That moment was, he now reflects, &#8220;the damning mistake.&#8221;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EBut that manipulation wasn&#8217;t the only one. He now confesses that there are other images from that project that were also altered using post-production techniques, and he says he also &#8220;appropriated photos&#8221; from colleagues like \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.facebook.com\u002Fdaniele.volpe.566\u002Fposts\u002F1908197796109484?pnref=story\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EDaniele Volpe\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, Hazel Thompson and Raul Irani, and lied in order to conceal those actions.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cdiv class=\"video-container\"\u003E\u003Cvideo id=\"brightcove-player-5077573618001\" data-video-id=\"5077573618001\" data-account=\"293884104\" data-player=\"SJa0Thl7\" data-embed=\"default\" autoplay controls preload=\"auto\" class=\"video-js responsive-video\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fvideo\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EDatta granted TIME his first interview since the scandal broke.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ETIME: What happened?\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESouvid Datta:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E The first thing I want to do is take responsibility. In 2013-15, [when I was] aged 22-24, I foolishly doctored images, inexcusably lied about others&rsquo; work being my own and then buried these wrongdoings in the years that followed. Now these images are resurfacing, they threaten to undermine any work I have legitimately pursued since and, crucially, all the trust that the people in my photos, my collaborators and supporting institutions placed in me. I am so profusely sorry for this. I hope to begin making amends.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EBut how exactly did an image by Mary Ellen Mark from 1978 end up in one of your photographs from 2014?\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn late 2013, I was 22 and still at University; I used to volunteer at a few NGOs that had a presence in the red-light district of Sonagachi in Kolkata. Around this time I also won my first digital camera and began exploring photography as a hobby. I didn&#8217;t know anything of photographic ethics, about the existence of a serious photojournalism industry or how best to investigate topics as a journalist. But I did come from a background of visual arts and I felt compelled to make images of my experiences in Kolkata, having been especially moved by the stories of the girls I met in Sonagachi.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOne girl in particular, Radhika, 17, was working in a brothel where the NGO helped young mothers. We spoke for some time over the course of the few days I spent there. She told me of her past, of her current problems, and also of her mentor: an older woman named Asma, who I met in passing. I photographed Radhika going about her daily activities, but Asma did not want her own photo taken. There was an instance in her room one afternoon, where the two were getting ready together, along with a friend. This moment spoke to their relationship as Radhika had described, but I did not take the image. I waited till after Asma left and shot a few frames of Radhika and her friend alone.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cfigure class=\"inline-portrait_small_2x aligncenter \"\u003E\u003Cimg data-attachment-id=\"4767201\" data-permalink=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002F4766312\u002Fsouvid-datta\u002Ftransvestites-getting-dressed-in-a-courtyard\u002F\" data-orig-file=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftimedotcom.files.wordpress.com\u002F2017\u002F05\u002Fmary-ellen-mark.jpg?quality=85\" data-orig-size=\"2500,1667\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta='{\"aperture\":\"0\",\"credit\":\"\",\"camera\":\"Primescan\",\"caption\":\"Transvestites getting dressed in a courtyard.\",\"created_timestamp\":\"1351780978\",\"copyright\":\"All rights Mary Ellen Mark\",\"focal_length\":\"0\",\"iso\":\"0\",\"shutter_speed\":\"0\",\"title\":\"Transvestites getting dressed in a courtyard.\",\"orientation\":\"1\"}' data-image-title=\"Transvestites getting dressed in a courtyard.\" data-image-description=\"pTransvestites getting dressed in a courtyard. 1978.\u002Fp\n\" data-medium-file=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftimedotcom.files.wordpress.com\u002F2017\u002F05\u002Fmary-ellen-mark.jpg?quality=85&w=300\" data-large-file=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftimedotcom.files.wordpress.com\u002F2017\u002F05\u002Fmary-ellen-mark.jpg?quality=85&w=600\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftimedotcom.files.wordpress.com\u002F2017\u002F05\u002Fmary-ellen-mark.jpg?w=560&#038;quality=85&#038;h=373\" alt=\"Transvestites getting dressed in a courtyard.\" height=\"373\"\u003E\n\u003Cfigcaption class=\"image-caption\" \u003E\u003Cspan class=\"credit\" \u003EMary Ellen Mark\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan class=\"caption\" \u003ETransvestites getting dressed in a courtyard. 1978.\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigcaption\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWeeks later, back in London and at University, while trying to learn post-production techniques on YouTube and starting my first photo blog, I came across the work of Mary Ellen Mark. Spotting the similarities in subject but without much further thought, I considered combining one of her images with my own as an experiment. I spotted a character in her work that particularly resembled Asma and for my own curiosity, in trying to recreate the picture I couldn&#8217;t make in reality, I tried placing her into the image next to Radhika. The damning mistake came in uploading that image onto my blog. I did this without accreditation or acknowledgment that it had been tampered with and that it included elements of [Mark&#8217;s] image. I wrote the caption as if Asma herself was in this image, not a woman from someone else&rsquo;s work. In effect, I lied.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWhy did you do it?\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EMy intention was not to profit from the inclusion of Mary Ellen Mark&rsquo;s work, but rather to see what it might have looked like had I somehow managed to persuade Asma to participate. I was frustrated that I hadn&rsquo;t. In part, I was also discovering the technology of Photoshop for the first time (as is clear in the result) and the creation of something new excited me. It felt like a very basic artistic achievement. There are other images from my initial shoots in Kolkata, not intended as journalistic work, which have also been altered using post-production techniques.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ECrucially, this was all done without the consideration of factual accuracy, ethical representation and journalistic responsibility that I came to learn of properly in the years to come. I didn&rsquo;t understand what a photojournalist was for a long time, let alone the weight of trying to assume that title.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWhy did you then go on to publish that photograph?\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EValidation and exposure are things I continue to struggle with today as a freelancer, but earlier I did seek after them more actively. I loved photography but initially the images on my blog had no coherent theme or narrative and were shot with little deliberate use in mind. So when a publication contacted me interested in using them, I felt overwhelmed and it seemed a tempting surprise. I rashly accepted the opportunity. I did not grasp immediately that this level of thoughtlessness was grossly selfish and deeply disrespectful to Ellen Mark&rsquo;s imagery and, above all, to the people I had photographed.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EDuring this same period, there were other lapses of judgment where I used imagery without acknowledgment, including that of Hazel Thompson and Raul Irani&rsquo;s work. Two of their images, along with those which I altered, were also included in my submissions for early photography competitions in 2014, though not published commercially anywhere.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EI cannot begin to say how much I regret having acted in this abhorrent, short-sighted and irresponsible manner. A few months after those images were published, I began to realize how reckless I had been, but by this point it felt too late to turn back. To think that mistakes like these would ever go unnoticed is a harsh lesson I am learning the dire truth of now.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWhen you realized what you had done, why didn&rsquo;t you try to own up to that action before today?\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAs I learned more about being a photojournalist, I grew ashamed of what I had done during more desperate moments of my life. And as my embarrassment deepened, I suppressed it in the foolish hope that those mistakes would fade away from memory &ndash; deleting appropriated photos [\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.facebook.com\u002Fdaniele.volpe.566\u002Fposts\u002F1908197796109484?pnref=story\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Efrom photographer Daniele Volpe\u003C\u002Fa\u003E] from early trips to Guatemala for instance, but not coming clean publicly. I will be the first to admit that it is terribly sad that it has taken this kind of exposure for me to step forward.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cdiv class=\"time-embed time-embed__facebook \" data-provider=\"facebook\" data-url=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.facebook.com\u002Fdaniele.volpe.566\u002Fposts\u002F1908197796109484?pnref=story\"\u003E\n\u003Cdiv class=\"fb-post\" data-href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.facebook.com\u002Fdaniele.volpe.566\u002Fposts\u002F1908197796109484?pnref=story\" data-width=\"500\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\n\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EAre there any other instances where you&rsquo;ve manipulated a scene, either during a shoot or in post-production?\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESeveral of my earliest photos from India between 2013-14 include elements of stitching and cloning, sometimes rehashing components from multiple frames.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn my very first journalistic endeavor in China, my editors warned me of going too far with certain post-production techniques, of over-saturating and vignetting; they eventually guided me through organizing my archives and proper captioning practices. Through 2014-15 onward, I learned to shoot in line with technical and ethical guidelines of my publisher and I am now diligent and up-front in case I ever decide to deviate by using non-traditional techniques such as double exposures or participatory photography. There is a world of difference in the projects I shoot now compared to those of a few years ago &#8211; both in conceptual approach, journalist rigor and style. I stay clear of manipulation, unless the client requests it for commercial intent.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EIn the last two years, you&rsquo;ve spent a lot of time producing deeply researched documentary work on human trafficking. Can we still trust that work?\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ETaking the seed of an idea I had interacting with girls in Sonagachi as a student, I spent a large part of the following three years developing an investigation into child sex trafficking across West Bengal; I covered human interest stories in the U.K. and in post-conflict zones across Afghanistan and Iraq; I followed the refugee influx across Europe in 2015 as sentiments around migration rose. From here on, I do not know what will happen to me or the stories I have followed. I fear above all that they may remain untold. My credibility has been fundamentally challenged, and I understand the serious implications of that in an industry where credibility counts for everything.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EI will say that for the work I have done as a serious photojournalist, and most of all for this project investigating women trafficking in India, I have given my utmost to uphold principles of respect, journalistic insight, compassion, perspective and perseverance. On top of this, multiple editorial teams have now vetted the project &mdash; from research to fact checking, to examining unedited files (images and video) to involving legal counsel and using a dedicated writer.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EDo you understand why your colleagues might be angry at you?\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe anger I face is entirely deserved. This incident has broken at a time when faith in committed reporters and accurate news is challenged more than ever. I truly dread contributing to any distrust that people have toward genuinely important news and imagery, and I deeply regret having compromised the faith, support and time that my friends, editors, institutions and publishers have offered me. Second chances aren&rsquo;t always deserved and I know it&rsquo;s up to me to earn mine and fight to regain any credibility, within or outside of this field. I let a lot of people down by hiding these past mistakes, and parts of me betrayed the trust of those closest and most supportive of me. I am sorry for this, truly.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cdiv class=\"time-embed time-embed__facebook \" data-provider=\"facebook\" data-url=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.facebook.com\u002FAlexiaFoundation\u002Fposts\u002F10154440197181524\"\u003E\n\u003Cdiv class=\"fb-post\" data-href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.facebook.com\u002FAlexiaFoundation\u002Fposts\u002F10154440197181524\" data-width=\"500\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\n\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EAs a freelance photographer, do you feel pressure from media organizations to attain a certain perfection that might tempt some to find shortcuts?\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EBeing a freelance photojournalist today is to live in an uncertain world of fierce competition &mdash; not only regarding photographic skill, but also of networking, self-promotion, business acumen, sincerity and flair. I certainly won&rsquo;t speak for others, but I have been affected by these industry pressures more than I would have ever liked to admit; resorting to extreme, foolhardy measures in the insecure hope of standing out. Perhaps one feels the need to cover a dangerous conflict zone, perhaps one succumbs to cloning out a distracting element in an image: these are unfortunately more common concerns than our industry wants to openly admit. On some level I hope this can be a prime lesson for others in exactly what not to do.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAt the end of the day, no one has forced me to act in a certain way. The burden of responsibility for all these mistakes lies with no one other than myself. I failed terribly and only I can make things right.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EHave you spoken to the subjects of your reportage since this came to light? Are you planning to?\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EYes, I am currently in the process of reaching out to the people involved in my stories. Many are friends and I owe each person I have ever shot a debt for having shared their time, their testimonies and parts of their lives with me. I must now be clear about my wrongdoings and the potential damage to their testimonies that these mistakes will cause. For those whose stories remain to be published, I stand deeply committed to getting these seen as promised &ndash; however that may be possible in the future &ndash; and I will be working hard to ensure that they receive the attention they deserve.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EThis interview has been edited for clarity.\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EOlivier Laurent \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003Eis the editor of TIME LightBox. Follow him on \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftwitter.com\u002Folivierclaurent\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ETwitter\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Finstagram.com\u002Folivierclaurent\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EInstagram\u003C\u002Fa\u003E @olivierclaurent\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EFollow \u003Cstrong\u003ETIME LightBox\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E on \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.facebook.com\u002Ftimelightbox\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EFacebook\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftwitter.com\u002Ftimepictures\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ETwitter\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Finstagram.com\u002Ftimelightbox\u002F\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EInstagram\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n","structured":[{"tag":"p","attributes":[],"content":["Souvid Datta, an award-winning photojournalist, admits it: he doctored images and infringed on the work of other photographers."]},{"tag":"p","attributes":[],"content":["Until this week, Datta was an example of stand-out early success in his field. I certainly wont speak for others, but I have been affected by these industry pressures more than I would have ever liked to admit; resorting to extreme, foolhardy measures in the insecure hope of standing out. Perhaps one feels the need to cover a dangerous conflict zone, perhaps one succumbs to cloning out a distracting element in an image: these are unfortunately more common concerns than our industry wants to openly admit. On some level I hope this can be a prime lesson for others in exactly what not to do."]},{"tag":"p","attributes":[],"content":["At the end of the day, no one has forced me to act in a certain way. Follow him on ",{"tag":"a","attributes":{"href":"http:\u002F\u002Ftwitter.com\u002Folivierclaurent","target":"_blank","rel":"noopener noreferrer"},"content":["Twitter"]}," and ",{"tag":"a","attributes":{"href":"http:\u002F\u002Finstagram.com\u002Folivierclaurent","target":"_blank","rel":"noopener noreferrer"},"content":["Instagram"]}," @olivierclaurent"]}]},{"tag":"p","attributes":[],"content":[{"tag":"em","attributes":[],"content":["Follow ",{"tag":"strong","attributes":[],"content":["TIME LightBox"]}," on ",{"tag":"a","attributes":{"href":"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.facebook.com\u002Ftimelightbox","target":"_blank","rel":"noopener noreferrer"},"content":["Facebook"]},", ",{"tag":"a","attributes":{"href":"https:\u002F\u002Ftwitter.com\u002Ftimepictures","target":"_blank","rel":"noopener noreferrer"},"content":["Twitter"]}," and ",{"tag":"a","attributes":{"href":"https:\u002F\u002Finstagram.com\u002Ftimelightbox\u002F","target":"_blank","rel":"noopener noreferrer"},"content":["Instagram"]},"."]}]}],"plain":"Souvid Datta, an award-winning photojournalist, admits it: he doctored images and infringed on the work of other photographers. Until this week, Datta was an example of stand-out early success in his field. He had won a Getty Images Editorial Grant, an Alexia Foundation award and the Visura Photojournalism Grant, highlights in a career that began about three years ago, when Datta was in Kolkata, India, producing a photographic project on violence in the city&rsquo;s sex industry. Now, however, he finds himself at the center of the latest photo-manipulation scandal to have ensnared the photojournalism community &mdash; a sorry trend that has also touched Paolo Pellegrin, Steve McCurry, Narciso Contreras and Giovanni Troilo, among others. As was first revealed by PetaPixel on Wednesday, it was during the course of that project, as he followed a girl in one of the town&rsquo;s brothels, that Datta turned to Photoshop. When the girl&#8217;s mentor asked not to be photographed, he cloned out a subject from a photograph by the legendary Mary Ellen Mark and pasted it in his own photograph. When he uploaded the image to his blog, there was no indication that the work was not entirely his own. That moment was, he now reflects, &#8220;the damning mistake.&#8221; But that manipulation wasn&#8217;t the only one. He now confesses that there are other images from that project that were also altered using post-production techniques, and he says he also &#8220;appropriated photos&#8221; from colleagues like Daniele Volpe, Hazel Thompson and Raul Irani, and lied in order to conceal those actions. Datta granted TIME his first interview since the scandal broke. TIME: What happened? Souvid Datta: The first thing I want to do is take responsibility. In 2013-15, [when I was] aged 22-24, I foolishly doctored images, inexcusably lied about others&rsquo; work being my own and then buried these wrongdoings in the years that followed. Now these images are resurfacing, they threaten to undermine any work I have legitimately pursued since and, crucially, all the trust that the people in my photos, my collaborators and supporting institutions placed in me. I am so profusely sorry for this. I hope to begin making amends. But how exactly did an image by Mary Ellen Mark from 1978 end up in one of your photographs from 2014? In late 2013, I was 22 and still at University; I used to volunteer at a few NGOs that had a presence in the red-light district of Sonagachi in Kolkata. Around this time I also won my first digital camera and began exploring photography as a hobby. I didn&#8217;t know anything of photographic ethics, about the existence of a serious photojournalism industry or how best to investigate topics as a journalist. But I did come from a background of visual arts and I felt compelled to make images of my experiences in Kolkata, having been especially moved by the stories of the girls I met in Sonagachi. One girl in particular, Radhika, 17, was working in a brothel where the NGO helped young mothers. We spoke for some time over the course of the few days I spent there. She told me of her past, of her current problems, and also of her mentor: an older woman named Asma, who I met in passing. I photographed Radhika going about her daily activities, but Asma did not want her own photo taken. There was an instance in her room one afternoon, where the two were getting ready together, along with a friend. This moment spoke to their relationship as Radhika had described, but I did not take the image. I waited till after Asma left and shot a few frames of Radhika and her friend alone. Mary Ellen MarkTransvestites getting dressed in a courtyard. 1978. Weeks later, back in London and at University, while trying to learn post-production techniques on YouTube and starting my first photo blog, I came across the work of Mary Ellen Mark. Spotting the similarities in subject but without much further thought, I considered combining one of her images with my own as an experiment. I spotted a character in her work that particularly resembled Asma and for my own curiosity, in trying to recreate the picture I couldn&#8217;t make in reality, I tried placing her into the image next to Radhika. The damning mistake came in uploading that image onto my blog. I did this without accreditation or acknowledgment that it had been tampered with and that it included elements of [Mark&#8217;s] image. I wrote the caption as if Asma herself was in this image, not a woman from someone else&rsquo;s work. In effect, I lied. Why did you do it? My intention was not to profit from the inclusion of Mary Ellen Mark&rsquo;s work, but rather to see what it might have looked like had I somehow managed to persuade Asma to participate. I was frustrated that I hadn&rsquo;t. In part, I was also discovering the technology of Photoshop for the first time (as is clear in the result) and the creation of something new excited me. It felt like a very basic artistic achievement. There are other images from my initial shoots in Kolkata, not intended as journalistic work, which have also been altered using post-production techniques. Crucially, this was all done without the consideration of factual accuracy, ethical representation and journalistic responsibility that I came to learn of properly in the years to come. I didn&rsquo;t understand what a photojournalist was for a long time, let alone the weight of trying to assume that title. Why did you then go on to publish that photograph? Validation and exposure are things I continue to struggle with today as a freelancer, but earlier I did seek after them more actively. I loved photography but initially the images on my blog had no coherent theme or narrative and were shot with little deliberate use in mind. So when a publication contacted me interested in using them, I felt overwhelmed and it seemed a tempting surprise. I rashly accepted the opportunity. I did not grasp immediately that this level of thoughtlessness was grossly selfish and deeply disrespectful to Ellen Mark&rsquo;s imagery and, above all, to the people I had photographed. During this same period, there were other lapses of judgment where I used imagery without acknowledgment, including that of Hazel Thompson and Raul Irani&rsquo;s work. Two of their images, along with those which I altered, were also included in my submissions for early photography competitions in 2014, though not published commercially anywhere. I cannot begin to say how much I regret having acted in this abhorrent, short-sighted and irresponsible manner. A few months after those images were published, I began to realize how reckless I had been, but by this point it felt too late to turn back. To think that mistakes like these would ever go unnoticed is a harsh lesson I am learning the dire truth of now. When you realized what you had done, why didn&rsquo;t you try to own up to that action before today? As I learned more about being a photojournalist, I grew ashamed of what I had done during more desperate moments of my life. And as my embarrassment deepened, I suppressed it in the foolish hope that those mistakes would fade away from memory &ndash; deleting appropriated photos [from photographer Daniele Volpe] from early trips to Guatemala for instance, but not coming clean publicly. I will be the first to admit that it is terribly sad that it has taken this kind of exposure for me to step forward. Are there any other instances where you&rsquo;ve manipulated a scene, either during a shoot or in post-production? Several of my earliest photos from India between 2013-14 include elements of stitching and cloning, sometimes rehashing components from multiple frames. In my very first journalistic endeavor in China, my editors warned me of going too far with certain post-production techniques, of over-saturating and vignetting; they eventually guided me through organizing my archives and proper captioning practices. Through 2014-15 onward, I learned to shoot in line with technical and ethical guidelines of my publisher and I am now diligent and up-front in case I ever decide to deviate by using non-traditional techniques such as double exposures or participatory photography. There is a world of difference in the projects I shoot now compared to those of a few years ago &#8211; both in conceptual approach, journalist rigor and style. I stay clear of manipulation, unless the client requests it for commercial intent. In the last two years, you&rsquo;ve spent a lot of time producing deeply researched documentary work on human trafficking. Can we still trust that work? Taking the seed of an idea I had interacting with girls in Sonagachi as a student, I spent a large part of the following three years developing an investigation into child sex trafficking across West Bengal; I covered human interest stories in the U.K. and in post-conflict zones across Afghanistan and Iraq; I followed the refugee influx across Europe in 2015 as sentiments around migration rose. From here on, I do not know what will happen to me or the stories I have followed. I fear above all that they may remain untold. My credibility has been fundamentally challenged, and I understand the serious implications of that in an industry where credibility counts for everything. I will say that for the work I have done as a serious photojournalist, and most of all for this project investigating women trafficking in India, I have given my utmost to uphold principles of respect, journalistic insight, compassion, perspective and perseverance. On top of this, multiple editorial teams have now vetted the project &mdash; from research to fact checking, to examining unedited files (images and video) to involving legal counsel and using a dedicated writer. Do you understand why your colleagues might be angry at you? The anger I face is entirely deserved. This incident has broken at a time when faith in committed reporters and accurate news is challenged more than ever. I truly dread contributing to any distrust that people have toward genuinely important news and imagery, and I deeply regret having compromised the faith, support and time that my friends, editors, institutions and publishers have offered me. Second chances aren&rsquo;t always deserved and I know it&rsquo;s up to me to earn mine and fight to regain any credibility, within or outside of this field. I let a lot of people down by hiding these past mistakes, and parts of me betrayed the trust of those closest and most supportive of me. I am sorry for this, truly. As a freelance photographer, do you feel pressure from media organizations to attain a certain perfection that might tempt some to find shortcuts? Being a freelance photojournalist today is to live in an uncertain world of fierce competition &mdash; not only regarding photographic skill, but also of networking, self-promotion, business acumen, sincerity and flair. I certainly won&rsquo;t speak for others, but I have been affected by these industry pressures more than I would have ever liked to admit; resorting to extreme, foolhardy measures in the insecure hope of standing out. Perhaps one feels the need to cover a dangerous conflict zone, perhaps one succumbs to cloning out a distracting element in an image: these are unfortunately more common concerns than our industry wants to openly admit. On some level I hope this can be a prime lesson for others in exactly what not to do. At the end of the day, no one has forced me to act in a certain way. The burden of responsibility for all these mistakes lies with no one other than myself. I failed terribly and only I can make things right. Have you spoken to the subjects of your reportage since this came to light? Are you planning to? Yes, I am currently in the process of reaching out to the people involved in my stories. Many are friends and I owe each person I have ever shot a debt for having shared their time, their testimonies and parts of their lives with me. I must now be clear about my wrongdoings and the potential damage to their testimonies that these mistakes will cause. For those whose stories remain to be published, I stand deeply committed to getting these seen as promised &ndash; however that may be possible in the future &ndash; and I will be working hard to ensure that they receive the attention they deserve. This interview has been edited for clarity. Olivier Laurent is the editor of TIME LightBox. Follow him on Twitter and Instagram @olivierclaurent Follow TIME LightBox on Facebook, Twitter and Instagram."},"featured_media":4767077,"comment_status":"closed","ping_status":"closed","categories":[1],"tags":[430957017,430957015,430957018,5601642,430957014,436,430957013,44925495,1615,67520879,430957016],"paid":false,"display_modified_time":false,"_links":{"fortune:featured":[{"embeddable":true,"href":"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002Fwp-json\u002Fti-api\u002Fv1\u002Ftime_collection_post\u002F4766312\u002Ffeatured"}],"fortune:primary_tag":[{"embeddable":true,"href":"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002Fwp-json\u002Fti-api\u002Fv1\u002Ftime_collection_post\u002F4766312\u002Fprimary_tag"}],"fortune:primary_section":[{"embeddable":true,"href":"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002Fwp-json\u002Fti-api\u002Fv1\u002Ftime_collection_post\u002F4766312\u002Fprimary_section"}]},"_embedded":{"fortune:featured":[{"size":"landscape","type":"image","visible":true,"image":{"id":4767077,"link":"https:\u002F\u002Ftimedotcom.files.wordpress.com\u002F2017\u002F05\u002Fsouvid-datta.jpg?quality=85&zoom=2","alt":"","caption":"Souvid Americans Have Cell Phones Than Landlines for the First Time"},"content":{"rendered":"\u003Cp\u003E(NEW YORK) &mdash; Deborah Braswell, a university administrator in Alabama, is a member of a dwindling group &mdash; people with a landline phone at home.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAccording to a U.S. government study released Thursday, 50.8 percent of homes and apartments had only cellphone service in the latter half of 2016, the first time such households attained a majority in the survey. Braswell and her family are part of the 45.9 percent that still have landline phones. The remaining households have no phone service at all.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EMore than 39 percent of U.S. households &mdash; including Braswell&#8217;s &mdash; have both landline and cellphone service. The landline comes in handy when someone misplaces one of the seven cellphones kicking around her three-story house in a Birmingham suburb. &#8220;You walk around your house calling yourself to find it,&#8221; she says.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIt&#8217;s also useful when someone breaks or loses a cellphone and has to wait for a replacement.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EThe patterns\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ERenters and younger adults are more likely to have just a cellphone, which researchers attribute to their mobility and comfort with newer technologies.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe in-person survey of 19,956 households was part of the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention&#8217;s National Health Interview Survey, which tracks landline use in order to assure representative samples in ongoing health studies. The survey has a margin of error of plus or minus 1 percentage point.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ECellphone-only home have other commonalities. &#8220;Wireless-only adults are more likely to drink heavily, more likely to smoke and be uninsured,&#8221; even after factoring for age and income, says Stephen J. According to a U.S. government study released Thursday, 50.8 percent of homes and apartments had only cellphone service in the latter half of 2016, the first time such households attained a majority in the survey. Braswell and her family are part of the 45.9 percent that still have landline phones. The remaining households have no phone service at all. More than 39 percent of U.S. households &mdash; including Braswell&#8217;s &mdash; have both landline and cellphone service. The landline comes in handy when someone misplaces one of the seven cellphones kicking around her three-story house in a Birmingham suburb. &#8220;You walk around your house calling yourself to find it,&#8221; she says. It&#8217;s also useful when someone breaks or loses a cellphone and has to wait for a replacement. The patterns Renters and younger adults are more likely to have just a cellphone, which researchers attribute to their mobility and comfort with newer technologies. The in-person survey of 19,956 households was part of the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention&#8217;s National Health Interview Survey, which tracks landline use in order to assure representative samples in ongoing health studies. The survey has a margin of error of plus or minus 1 percentage point. Cellphone-only home have other commonalities. &#8220;Wireless-only adults are more likely to drink heavily, more likely to smoke and be uninsured,&#8221; even after factoring for age and income, says Stephen J. Blumberg, the study&#8217;s co-author (and a landline user himself). &#8220;There certainly is something about giving up a landline that appeals to the same people who may engage in risky behavior.&#8221; Why that&#8217;s so will require further research. The survey doesn&#8217;t get into why people ditch or keep landlines, though landline users cited a number of reasons for hanging on in phone interviews and email exchanges with The Associated Press. No choice in the matter Plenty of people would get rid of their landlines if they could. It goes beyond complaints about cellular reception at home. Joe Krkoska, a supply chain director, needs a traditional copper wire for his home security system in Zionsville, Indiana. Getting rid of the line would require crews to drill holes in his home and put batteries in the bedroom. No thanks, he says. Chris Houchens, who works in sales and marketing, says his phone company forces him to get a landline with internet service. There&#8217;s no cable TV alternative where he lives in rural Smiths Grove, Kentucky. And those who could drop phone service might pay more after losing package discounts. Martin Axel, a retired hospital administrator in Seal Beach, California, says dropping the landline would increase his cable bill by more than $40 a month. Emergencies Traditional copper phone lines have their own power supply, so those landlines still work during blackouts. Internet-based phones through the cable or phone company aren&#8217;t true landlines, although the CDC counts them that way. The internet modem for these phones still needs power. Both kinds of landline phones are more dependable for 911. Even if you can&#8217;t give dispatchers your home address, they would often have that already. Cellphones primarily use GPS for location, which means the dispatcher might know which building you&#8217;re in, but not the specific apartment. For that reason, Trey Forgety of the nonprofit National Emergency Number Association recommends landlines for those who live alone and have a disability or medical condition. He says cellphone location accuracy is improving, but there&#8217;s still work to be done. Spam magnets In many households, the landline is a honey trap for telemarketers. &#8220;We never use the landline, and the only calls I get on it are from someone looking to sell me something,&#8221; says Matt Lawrence, a management consultant in O&#8217;Fallon, Missouri. On the other hand, it&#8217;s comforting to have a device just for calls &mdash; without &#8220;all of the irritating bells and whistles of smartphones,&#8221; says Brad Cooney, a Navy veteran in Brandon, Mississippi. &#8220;I can shut the (cell) phone off and still have a landline if someone needs to call me.&#8221; Phone nostalgia The landline harkens to an era in which a number is tied to a family and not an individual. You can call your parents&#8217; home and not play favorites in choosing Mom or Dad. Children can talk to Grandma at once from separate phone extensions. &#8220;My parents had landlines, as did their parents,&#8221; says Axel, the landline user in Seal Beach. &#8220;It&#8217;s probably a habit. It just feels more comfortable to me.&#8221; Cynthia Dibblee, a retired teacher in Merced, California, has elderly parents who &#8220;can&#8217;t remember our cell numbers but know the landline by heart.&#8221; The future USTelecom, a trade group for traditional phone companies, estimates that true landlines &mdash; the copper kind &mdash; now connect fewer than 20 percent of households. The group says companies have adapted by offering other types of services, including video and, for some, cellphones. Even so, phone companies get new landline customers now and then. Shawn Fisch, a 37-year-old teacher in New York, got his first landline after becoming a dad. When his son is old enough, he says, he&#8217;ll need &#8220;an extra way to phone home.&#8221;"},"featured_media":2966661,"comment_status":"closed","ping_status":"closed","categories":[1],"tags":[430939235,38616],"paid":false,"display_modified_time":false,"_links":{"fortune:featured":[{"embeddable":true,"href":"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002Fwp-json\u002Fti-api\u002Fv1\u002Ftime_collection_post\u002F4766983\u002Ffeatured"}],"fortune:primary_tag":[{"embeddable":true,"href":"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002Fwp-json\u002Fti-api\u002Fv1\u002Ftime_collection_post\u002F4766983\u002Fprimary_tag"}],"fortune:primary_section":[{"embeddable":true,"href":"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002Fwp-json\u002Fti-api\u002Fv1\u002Ftime_collection_post\u002F4766983\u002Fprimary_section"}]},"_embedded":{"fortune:featured":[{"size":"landscape","type":"image","visible":true,"image":{"id":2966661,"link":"https:\u002F\u002Ftimedotcom.files.wordpress.com\u002F2014\u002F07\u002F494842237.jpg?quality=85&zoom=2","alt":"Technological reshape health care in the United States and dramatically reduce the number of Americans with health insurance.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003EIf the Republican bill passes in the Senate, it will reorganize insurance markets and affect coverage for many millions of Americans.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&#8220;Seven years of Obamacare is enough,&#8221; House Speaker Paul Ryan \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftwitter.com\u002FSpeakerRyan\u002Fstatus\u002F860130492582158336\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Ewrote\u003C\u002Fa\u003E on Twitter Thursday morning.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe vote comes nearly six weeks after House Republicans had to \u003Ca conservatives in their caucus. The bill has \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002F4712852\u002Fahca-donald-trump-obamacare-repeal-failure\u002F\"\u003Esince been amended\u003C\u002Fa\u003E twice, though the broad outlines remain the same.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cdiv class=\"video-container\"\u003E\u003Cvideo id=\"brightcove-player-5405300384001\" data-video-id=\"5405300384001\" data-account=\"293884104\" data-player=\"SJa0Thl7\" data-embed=\"default\" controls preload=\"auto\" class=\"video-js responsive-video\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fvideo\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe bill \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002F4762385\u002Fahca-health-care-preexisting-conditions-facts\u002F\"\u003Eweakens protections\u003C\u002Fa\u003E for people with pre-existing medical conditions.
