It also significantly reduces federal assistance to lower-income Americans paying for health insurance. It also significantly reduces federal assistance to lower-income Americans paying for health insurance, and it defunds Planned Parenthood.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn addition, it repeals the Obamacare &ldquo;individual mandate,&rdquo; the rule requiring people to buy insurance.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMORE:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E \u003Cem\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002F4766850\u002Fhealth-care-vote-trumpcare-bill-specifics\u002F\"\u003ENo One Knows Exactly How Trumpcare Would Work\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003E&ldquo;This bill brings choice and competition back into the health care marketplace and puts health care decisions back in the hands of patients and doctors,&rdquo; Rep. Diane Black said on the floor of the House. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s been a winding road to get to this point, but we&rsquo;re here today to fulfill the promise we made to the American people.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ECalled the American Health Care Act, the bill was passed on Thursday morning without a score from the nonpartisan Congressional Budget Office, which is highly unusual for major legislation. But a similar, earlier version of the Republican bill would have \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002F4700051\u002Fobamacare-repeal-cbo-congressional-budget-office-analysis\u002F\"\u003Ereduced the number of insured by 24 million people\u003C\u002Fa\u003E by 2026 and raised premiums by 15 to 20% before they began to drop, according to the CBO.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003EDemocrats decried the bill, saying it was \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002F4766895\u002Fhealth-care-vote-republicans-process-quotes\u002F\"\u003Erushed through the House\u003C\u002Fa\u003E without enough review and would damage the health care system. &ldquo;Republicans are maliciously again attempting to destroy healthcare and coverage for the American people,&rdquo; said House Minority Leader Nancy Pelosi.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003EThe vote on Thursday morning was a nailbiter.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003EMany Republicans &mdash; including those who voted for it &mdash; were privately unhappy with health care bill. For conservatives who wanted to repeal Obamacare fully, it does not go far enough; for moderates, it is too harsh on lower- and middle-income Americans. The bill is a &ldquo;technocratic crap sandwich,&rdquo; one Republican lawmaker said.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003EIn addition, the bill comes without a score from the CBO, and some members felt the bill was rushed to the floor without members having time to understand the effects of the bill.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003ERep. Peter King of New York said he had not read the latest amendment just hours before the vote, but that he would vote in favor anyway based on his discussions with Republican leadership. &ldquo;You have to strike while you can,&rdquo; King said.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003EFor some, assurances from leadership were not enough. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m certainly not going to vote on a bill of this magnitude that hasn&rsquo;t been fully scored by the Congressional Budget Office and whose price tag is unknown,&rdquo; said Rep. Mike Coffman, Republican of Colorado.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003EBut Republicans in the House, embarrassed by their failed effort to pass the bill at the end of March, were determined to push the health care on to the Senate. It will almost certainly face a significant overhaul in the Senate, and then be sent to a joint-chamber conference committee, where changes are reconciled.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003ERepublican House members described the bill as a first-draft effort, necessary to begin the repeal of Obamacare. Waiting any longer, some Republicans said, could cause the bill to fail.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003E&ldquo;If we couldn&rsquo;t get this across the floor it would all stop here today,&rdquo; said Rep. Tom Cole of Oklahoma. &ldquo;Nobody should look at this as the be-all and end-all. It&rsquo;s the first step, not the last step.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003E&ldquo;I don&rsquo;t think time would be our friend. We want to get it over to the Senate so they can start their job,&rdquo; said Rep. Chris Collins of New York.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003EBut as a legislative blueprint, the House bill significantly weakens the protections established under Obamacare for Americans with preexisting conditions. It also will cut aid to lower-income Americans, making health care subsidies based on age, rather than income.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003EThose who stand to gain immediately include younger and healthier insurance buyers in the open marketplace, and the wealthiest taxpayers, who will see a significant tax cut.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003EThe farthest-reaching effect of the American Health Care Act, however, may be provisions that roll back the expansion of Medicaid beginning in 2018. Obamacare expanded Medicaid for states who chose to opt-in to everyone making up to 138% of the poverty line, expanding coverage in those states by well over 10 million people.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003EHalting the Medicaid expansion in those states, combined with the bill&rsquo;s restructuring of health insurance subsidies, will mean that people making minimum wage and slightly more will experience the sharpest drop in coverage.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003E&ldquo;The AHCA would lead to catastrophic coverage losses among those right above the poverty line,&rdquo; said Dr. Julie Donohue, director of the Medicaid Research Center at the University of Pittsburgh Health Policy Institute. &ldquo;While individuals right above poverty-level could technically purchase coverage on the marketplace, such coverage will be out of reach for nearly all.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003EPresident Trump has promised the bill would cover those with preexisting conditions, but the bill would allow states to let insurers charge people with preexisting conditions higher premiums.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp dir=\"ltr\"\u003EThursday&rsquo;s House vote ensures that the Republican effort to repeal Obamacare does not end. Most Americans dont know who their Congressperson is. But they will now."]}],"plain":"House Republicans voted on Thursday to repeal Obamacare, making good on a seven-year campaign promise that could reshape health care in the United States and dramatically reduce the number of Americans with health insurance. If the Republican bill passes in the Senate, it will reorganize insurance markets and affect coverage for many millions of Americans. &#8220;Seven years of Obamacare is enough,&#8221; House Speaker Paul Ryan wrote on Twitter Thursday morning. The vote comes nearly six weeks after House Republicans had to pull an earlier version due to disagreements between moderates and conservatives in their caucus. The bill has since been amended twice, though the broad outlines remain the same. The bill weakens protections for people with pre-existing medical conditions. It rolls back the expansion of Medicaid and cuts taxes on the wealthy. It also significantly reduces federal assistance to lower-income Americans paying for health insurance. It also significantly reduces federal assistance to lower-income Americans paying for health insurance, and it defunds Planned Parenthood. In addition, it repeals the Obamacare &ldquo;individual mandate,&rdquo; the rule requiring people to buy insurance. MORE: No One Knows Exactly How Trumpcare Would Work &ldquo;This bill brings choice and competition back into the health care marketplace and puts health care decisions back in the hands of patients and doctors,&rdquo; Rep. Diane Black said on the floor of the House. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s been a winding road to get to this point, but we&rsquo;re here today to fulfill the promise we made to the American people.&rdquo; Called the American Health Care Act, the bill was passed on Thursday morning without a score from the nonpartisan Congressional Budget Office, which is highly unusual for major legislation. But a similar, earlier version of the Republican bill would have reduced the number of insured by 24 million people by 2026 and raised premiums by 15 to 20% before they began to drop, according to the CBO. Democrats decried the bill, saying it was rushed through the House without enough review and would damage the health care system. &ldquo;Republicans are maliciously again attempting to destroy healthcare and coverage for the American people,&rdquo; said House Minority Leader Nancy Pelosi. The vote on Thursday morning was a nailbiter. Many Republicans &mdash; including those who voted for it &mdash; were privately unhappy with health care bill. For conservatives who wanted to repeal Obamacare fully, it does not go far enough; for moderates, it is too harsh on lower- and middle-income Americans. The bill is a &ldquo;technocratic crap sandwich,&rdquo; one Republican lawmaker said. In addition, the bill comes without a score from the CBO, and some members felt the bill was rushed to the floor without members having time to understand the effects of the bill. Rep. Peter King of New York said he had not read the latest amendment just hours before the vote, but that he would vote in favor anyway based on his discussions with Republican leadership. &ldquo;You have to strike while you can,&rdquo; King said. For some, assurances from leadership were not enough. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m certainly not going to vote on a bill of this magnitude that hasn&rsquo;t been fully scored by the Congressional Budget Office and whose price tag is unknown,&rdquo; said Rep. Mike Coffman, Republican of Colorado. But Republicans in the House, embarrassed by their failed effort to pass the bill at the end of March, were determined to push the health care on to the Senate. It will almost certainly face a significant overhaul in the Senate, and then be sent to a joint-chamber conference committee, where changes are reconciled. Republican House members described the bill as a first-draft effort, necessary to begin the repeal of Obamacare. Waiting any longer, some Republicans said, could cause the bill to fail. &ldquo;If we couldn&rsquo;t get this across the floor it would all stop here today,&rdquo; said Rep. Tom Cole of Oklahoma. &ldquo;Nobody should look at this as the be-all and end-all. It&rsquo;s the first step, not the last step.&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t think time would be our friend. We want to get it over to the Senate so they can start their job,&rdquo; said Rep. Chris Collins of New York. But as a legislative blueprint, the House bill significantly weakens the protections established under Obamacare for Americans with preexisting conditions. It also will cut aid to lower-income Americans, making health care subsidies based on age, rather than income. Those who stand to gain immediately include younger and healthier insurance buyers in the open marketplace, and the wealthiest taxpayers, who will see a significant tax cut. The farthest-reaching effect of the American Health Care Act, however, may be provisions that roll back the expansion of Medicaid beginning in 2018. Obamacare expanded Medicaid for states who chose to opt-in to everyone making up to 138% of the poverty line, expanding coverage in those states by well over 10 million people. Halting the Medicaid expansion in those states, combined with the bill&rsquo;s restructuring of health insurance subsidies, will mean that people making minimum wage and slightly more will experience the sharpest drop in coverage. &ldquo;The AHCA would lead to catastrophic coverage losses among those right above the poverty line,&rdquo; said Dr. Julie Donohue, director of the Medicaid Research Center at the University of Pittsburgh Health Policy Institute. &ldquo;While individuals right above poverty-level could technically purchase coverage on the marketplace, such coverage will be out of reach for nearly all.&rdquo; President Trump has promised the bill would cover those with preexisting conditions, but the bill would allow states to let insurers charge people with preexisting conditions higher premiums. Thursday&rsquo;s House vote ensures that the Republican effort to repeal Obamacare does not end. But the Republican victory on Thursday may be setting lawmakers up for a major defeat in 2018. The Republican health care bill is deeply unpopular, with just 17% of Americans approving of the bill, according to a poll from late March. The bill was opposed by AARP as well as the American Medical Association, health groups and hospitals. A majority of Americans want Congress to fix Obamacare rather than repeal it outright. Democrats believe the bill&rsquo;s passage on Thursday will help set up the party for a wave of congressional election victories next year. Even as they lambasted the Republican vote, Democrats were preparing to target the moderate Republicans in swing districts who voted for the bill. &#8220;You vote for this bill, you&#8217;re walking the plank,&#8221; House Minority Leader Nancy Pelosi told Republicans on the house floor. The bill &ldquo;is going to provide a great civics lesson for America,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;Most Americans don&rsquo;t know who their Congressperson is. That would cut off millions who had been covered by Medicaid for the first time. \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003EIt also cut taxes on the wealthy (the top 0.1% would see around $200,000 more each year), defunded Planned Parenthood and repealed the individual mandate in Obamacare.\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003EMany lawmakers&mdash;including significant numbers of Republicans&mdash;opposed the bill. It would reduce the deficit by just around $150 billion, a relatively small amount. \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003EThis plan was strongly opposed by groups ranging from the American Medical Association to the American Association of Retired People, as well as conservative groups. \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003EConservatives Republicans said it didn&rsquo;t do enough to lower premiums and repeal regulations, and moderate Republicans said it would hurt too many people who had benefited from Medicaid and the Obamacare premiums. \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003ERepublican leaders decided on March 31 \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002F4712852\u002Fahca-donald-trump-obamacare-repeal-failure\u002F\"\u003Enot to bring the bill\u003C\u002Fa\u003E to the floor for a vote when it became clear it would fail. \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003E\u003Cb\u003EThe MacArthur Amendment\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIt seemed at the start of April that Republicans had defeated their own effort to repeal Obamacare. But the pools have a shoddy history: most are underfunded and charge people as much as 200% more than they would pay for on the open market. \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003ENonetheless, in the last week of April, the MacArthur Amendment helped swing most of the near-40-member House Freedom Caucus to support the bill. \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003E\u003Cb\u003EThe Upton Amendment\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003EStill, however, there were numerous moderate Republicans who opposed the bill. The high-risk pools did not seem to be a stable enough protection, many argued, and they would not receive enough funding.\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003ERep. Fred Upton, a moderate congressman from Michigan who had previously opposed the bill, visited the White House on Wednesday and announced the most recent amendment: $8 billion in federal funding to shore up the high-risk pools. \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003EThat was enough to sway another member, Rep. Billy Long of Missouri, and as of Wednesday afternoon, several moderates said they could reconsider the bill. \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003EStill, the \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.nationalaffairs.com\u002Fpublications\u002Fdetail\u002Fhow-to-cover-pre-existing-conditions\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003E$8 billion will likely not be enough\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fthehill.com\u002Fpolicy\u002Fhealthcare\u002F331778-experts-new-gop-funding-on-pre-existing-conditions-not-nearly-enough\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003Eto cover shortfalls\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003E in the high-risk pools. \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003EAnd all of what moderates disliked about the first version of the bill will remain in place. \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight:400;\"\u003E&ldquo;It&rsquo;s still the underlying policy,&rdquo; said Rep. Ryan Costello, a moderate Republican who opposes the bill.\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ENow there is talk about a vote this week, before the bill can get a score from the Congressional Budget Office, as is customary.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe question is whether all the amendments were enough.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n","structured":[{"tag":"p","attributes":[],"content":["After numerous amendments and five months of public debate, the Republican bill to repeal Obamacare is closer than ever to passing the House of Representatives. "]},{"tag":"p","attributes":[],"content":["Still, the ",{"tag":"a","attributes":{"href":"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.nationalaffairs.com\u002Fpublications\u002Fdetail\u002Fhow-to-cover-pre-existing-conditions"},"content":["$8 billion will likely not be enough"]}," ",{"tag":"a","attributes":{"href":"http:\u002F\u002Fthehill.com\u002Fpolicy\u002Fhealthcare\u002F331778-experts-new-gop-funding-on-pre-existing-conditions-not-nearly-enough"},"content":["to cover shortfalls"]}," in the high-risk pools. Officials said Wednesday night that the House would vote on the bill on Thursday. President Trump and House Speaker Paul Ryan can afford 23 defections in the Republican ranks if they want to pass a bill. Currently, there are about 18 to 20 members who oppose the bill, according to different counts. That&rsquo;s at least two fewer &ldquo;no&rsquo;s&rdquo; than yesterday, and more could come on board in the next day. Here&#8217;s how the bill has changed over the past five weeks. American Health Care Act The first draft of the Republican bill, hammered out in large part by Ryan and finalized at the end of March, made two major changes to Obamacare. First, the bill cut subsidies to low- and middle-income individuals. Instead of basing the subsidies on income, the bill based the subsidies on age. Most people who rely on Obamacare subsidies would have seen their subsidies dramatically reduced. Older people get more subsidies than younger, or up to $4,000 per individual. For example, a middle-aged couple in rural North Carolina receiving $10,600 under Obamacare would receive just $6,500 under the Republican replacement. Many would have been unable to afford their plans. It would have helped healthy and young people, and disadvantaged sicker and older people. Second, the bill would phase out the expansion of Medicaid in states like Pennsylvania and Kentucky, which chose to raise the eligibility from the poverty line to 133% of the poverty line. That would cut off millions who had been covered by Medicaid for the first time. It also cut taxes on the wealthy (the top 0.1% would see around $200,000 more each year), defunded Planned Parenthood and repealed the individual mandate in Obamacare. Many lawmakers&mdash;including significant numbers of Republicans&mdash;opposed the bill. It would have increased the number of uninsured people by 24 million in 2026, according the nonpartisan Congressional Budget Office. It would cause premiums to rise by an average of 15% to 20% in the short-term, though they would eventually fall in the long term. It would reduce the deficit by just around $150 billion, a relatively small amount. This plan was strongly opposed by groups ranging from the American Medical Association to the American Association of Retired People, as well as conservative groups. Conservatives Republicans said it didn&rsquo;t do enough to lower premiums and repeal regulations, and moderate Republicans said it would hurt too many people who had benefited from Medicaid and the Obamacare premiums. Republican leaders decided on March 31 not to bring the bill to the floor for a vote when it became clear it would fail. The MacArthur Amendment It seemed at the start of April that Republicans had defeated their own effort to repeal Obamacare. But during the April recess, a moderate Republican from New Jersey, Rep. Tom MacArthur, hashed out an agreement with the arch-conservative House Freedom Caucus. The underlying bill would remain the same. In a new amendment, however, states would be allowed to opt out of a crucial Obamacare provision that protected people with pre-existing conditions from being charged higher prices. The so-called MacArthur Amendment said that insurance companies would still be required to cover people with pre-existing conditions; only now, they could charge them higher premiums. Some healthier people might see their premiums fall. But those with pre-existing conditions (52 million Americans are estimated to have a pre-existing condition, according to the Kaiser Family Foundation) would likely see their premiums rise dramatically, if they are buying insurance on the marketplace. In order to protect those with pre-existing conditions in states that opt-out of the Obamacare rule, there would be a &ldquo;high-risk pool&rdquo; to help cover individuals, backed up by federal funds. But the pools have a shoddy history: most are underfunded and charge people as much as 200% more than they would pay for on the open market. Nonetheless, in the last week of April, the MacArthur Amendment helped swing most of the near-40-member House Freedom Caucus to support the bill. The Upton Amendment Still, however, there were numerous moderate Republicans who opposed the bill. The MacArthur Amendment did not allay their underlying concerns; in fact, it pushed the bill farther to the right. The concern among moderates was that the bill would not adequately people those with pre-existing conditions. The high-risk pools did not seem to be a stable enough protection, many argued, and they would not receive enough funding. Rep. Fred Upton, a moderate congressman from Michigan who had previously opposed the bill, visited the White House on Wednesday and announced the most recent amendment: $8 billion in federal funding to shore up the high-risk pools. That was enough to sway another member, Rep. Billy Long of Missouri, and as of Wednesday afternoon, several moderates said they could reconsider the bill. Still, the $8 billion will likely not be enough to cover shortfalls in the high-risk pools. And all of what moderates disliked about the first version of the bill will remain in place. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s still the underlying policy,&rdquo; said Rep. Ryan Costello, a moderate Republican who opposes the bill. Now there is talk about a vote this week, before the bill can get a score from the Congressional Budget Office, as is customary. The question is whether all the amendments were
